<p><b>Roberta Joan "Joni" Mitchell</b>, <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Canada" title="Order of Canada">CC</a> (née <b>Anderson</b>; born November 7, 1943) is a Canadian <a href="/wiki/Singer-songwriter" title="Singer-songwriter">singer-songwriter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Record_producer" title="Record producer">record producer</a> and painter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <i>Rolling Stone</i> called her "one of the greatest songwriters ever",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wild_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-wild-2">[2]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a> has stated, "When the dust settles, Joni Mitchell may stand as the most important and influential female recording artist of the late 20th century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Drawing from folk, pop, rock and jazz, Mitchell's songs often reflect social and environmental ideals as well as her feelings about romance, confusion, disillusionment and joy.</p>
<p>Mitchell began singing in small nightclubs in <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a> and western Canada before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Busking" title="Busking">busking</a> in the streets and shoddy nightclubs of <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>. In 1965, she moved to the United States and began touring. Some of her original songs ("Urge for Going", "<a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Morning" title="Chelsea Morning">Chelsea Morning</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Both_Sides,_Now" title="Both Sides, Now">Both Sides, Now</a>", "The Circle Game") were covered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_singer" title="Folk singer">folk singers</a>, allowing her to sign with Reprise Records and record her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joni_Mitchell_(album)" title="Joni Mitchell (album)">debut album</a> in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Settling in <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">Southern California</a>, Mitchell, with popular songs like "<a href="/wiki/Big_Yellow_Taxi" title="Big Yellow Taxi">Big Yellow Taxi</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Woodstock_(song)" title="Woodstock (song)">Woodstock</a>", helped define an era and <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">a generation</a>. Her 1971 recording <i><a href="/wiki/Blue_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Blue (Joni Mitchell album)">Blue</a></i> was rated the 30th best album ever made in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone_(magazine)" title="Rolling Stone (magazine)"><i>Rolling Stone</i></a>'s list of the "<a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone%27s_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="Rolling Stone's 500 Greatest Albums of All Time">500 Greatest Albums of All Time</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Mitchell switched labels and began moving toward <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> rhythms by way of lush <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> textures on 1974's <i><a href="/wiki/Court_and_Spark" title="Court and Spark">Court and Spark</a></i>, her best-selling LP, featuring the radio hits "<a href="/wiki/Help_Me_(Joni_Mitchell_song)" title="Help Me (Joni Mitchell song)">Help Me</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Free_Man_in_Paris" title="Free Man in Paris">Free Man in Paris</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her wide-ranging <a href="/wiki/Contralto" title="Contralto">contralto</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> vocals and distinctive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_tuning" title="Open tuning">open-tuned</a> guitar and piano compositions grew more harmonically and rhythmically complex as she explored jazz, melding it with influences of <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%26B" title="R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a>, classical music, and <a href="/wiki/World_music" title="World music">non-western beats</a>. In the late 1970s, she began working closely with noted jazz musicians, among them <a href="/wiki/Jaco_Pastorius" title="Jaco Pastorius">Jaco Pastorius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wayne_Shorter" title="Wayne Shorter">Wayne Shorter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbie_Hancock" title="Herbie Hancock">Herbie Hancock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pat_Metheny" title="Pat Metheny">Pat Metheny</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mingus" title="Charles Mingus">Charles Mingus</a>, who asked her to collaborate on his final recordings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> She turned again toward pop, embraced <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic music</a>, and engaged in political protest.</p>
<p>She is the sole producer credited on most of her albums, including all her work in the 1970s. A blunt critic of the <a href="/wiki/Music_industry" title="Music industry">music industry</a>, she quit touring and released her 17th, and reportedly last, album of original songs in 2007.</p>
<p>With roots in visual art, Mitchell designed her own album covers. She describes herself as a "painter derailed by circumstance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mitchell was born Roberta Joan Anderson on November 7, 1943, in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Macleod" title="Fort Macleod">Fort Macleod</a>, Alberta, Canada, the daughter of Myrtle Marguerite (McKee) and William Andrew Anderson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Her mother's ancestors were <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irishtimes_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes-11">[11]</a></sup> her father was from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norwegian_people" title="Norwegian people">Norwegian</a> family (and possibly had some <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami</a> ancestors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-READ_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-READ-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Her mother was a teacher. Her father was a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Air_Force" title="Royal Canadian Air Force">Royal Canadian Air Force</a> <a href="/wiki/Flight_lieutenant" title="Flight lieutenant">flight lieutenant</a> who instructed new pilots at <a href="/wiki/RCAF_Station_Fort_Macleod" title="RCAF Station Fort Macleod">RCAF Station Fort Macleod</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied forces</a> were gathering to learn to fly during World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> During those years, she moved with her parents to various bases in western Canada. After the war, her father began working as a grocer, and his work took the family to <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>, to the towns of <a href="/wiki/Maidstone,_Saskatchewan" title="Maidstone, Saskatchewan">Maidstone</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Battleford" title="North Battleford">North Battleford</a>. She later sang about her small-town upbringing in "Song for Sharon".</p>
<p>In Maidstone they lived beside the railroad track, where Mitchell waved at the only train that passed through each day. Many of the town's residents were <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> people. Mitchell seemed athletic rather than academic, but still responded to her mother's love of literature and her father's love of music, and she briefly studied classical piano.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BRAND_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-BRAND-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>At age nine, Mitchell contracted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a> in an epidemic, and was hospitalised for weeks. No longer athletic, she turned her thoughts to her creative talent, and considered a singing or dancing career for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crowe_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-crowe-16">[16]</a></sup> By nine, she was a smoker; she denies claims that smoking has affected her voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>At 11, she moved with her family to the city of <a href="/wiki/Saskatoon" title="Saskatoon">Saskatoon</a>, which she considers her hometown. She responded badly to formal education, preferring a freethinking outlook, and was drawn to art, a pursuit often regarded as peripheral at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crowe_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-crowe-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> One unconventional teacher did manage to make an impact on her, stimulating her to write poetry, and her first album includes a dedication to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In twelfth grade, she flunked out (though she later picked up her studies) and hung out downtown with a rowdy set until deciding that she was getting too close to the criminal world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crowe_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-crowe-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>At this time, country music began to eclipse rock, and Mitchell wanted to play the guitar. As her mother disapproved of its hillbilly associations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> she settled initially for the <a href="/wiki/Ukulele" title="Ukulele">ukulele</a>. Eventually she taught herself guitar from a <a href="/wiki/Pete_Seeger" title="Pete Seeger">Pete Seeger</a> songbook,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> but the polio had affected her fingers, and she had to devise dozens of alternative tunings of her own. Later this improvised approach was "a tool to break free of standard approaches to harmony and structure" in her own songwriting.</p>
<p>Mitchell started singing with her friends at bonfires around <a href="/wiki/Waskesiu_Lake" title="Waskesiu Lake">Waskesiu Lake</a>. Her first paid performance was on October 31, 1962, at a Saskatoon club that featured folk and jazz performers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIGS_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIGS-22">[22]</a></sup> At 18, she widened her repertoire to include her own personal favorites like <a href="/wiki/%C3%89dith_Piaf" title="Édith Piaf">Édith Piaf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miles_Davis" title="Miles Davis">Miles Davis</a>. Though she never performed jazz herself in those days, she and her friends sought out gigs by jazz musicians. Mitchell said, "My jazz background began with one of the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambert,_Hendricks_and_Ross" title="Lambert, Hendricks and Ross">Lambert, Hendricks and Ross</a> albums." That album, <i><a href="/wiki/Lambert,_Hendricks,_%26_Ross!" title="Lambert, Hendricks, &amp; Ross!">The Hottest New Group in Jazz</a></i>, was hard to find in Canada, she says. "So I saved up and bought it at a bootleg price. I considered that album to be my Beatles. I learned every song off of it, and I don't think there is another album anywhere – including my own – on which I know every note and word of every song."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>But art was still her chief passion at this stage, and when she finished high school at <a href="/wiki/Aden_Bowman_Collegiate" title="Aden Bowman Collegiate">Aden Bowman Collegiate</a> in Saskatoon, she took art classes at the <a href="/wiki/Saskatoon_Technical_Collegiate" title="Saskatoon Technical Collegiate">Saskatoon Technical Collegiate</a> with abstract expressionist painter <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bonli" title="Henry Bonli">Henry Bonli</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeller200873_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeller200873-24">[24]</a></sup> and then left home to attend the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alberta_College_of_Art" title="Alberta College of Art">Alberta College of Art</a> in Calgary for the 1963–64 school year. Here she felt disillusioned about the high priority given to technical skill over free-class creativity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BRAND_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-BRAND-15">[15]</a></sup> and also felt out of step with the trend towards pure abstraction, and the tendency to move into commercial art. After a year, at age 20, she dropped out of school – a decision that much displeased her parents, who could remember the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> and valued education highly.</p>
<p>She had kept gigging as a folk musician on weekends, playing at her college and at a local hotel. Around this time she took a $15-a-week job in a Calgary coffeehouse, "singing long tragic songs in a minor key". She also sang at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hootenannies" title="Hootenannies">hootenannies</a> and even made appearances on some local TV and radio shows in Calgary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIGS_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-GIGS-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1964 at the age of 20, she told her mother that she intended to be a folk singer in Toronto, and she left western Canada for the first time in her life, heading east for Ontario. On the three-day train ride there, Mitchell wrote her first song, called "Day After Day". She also stopped at the Mariposa Folk Festival to see <a href="/wiki/Buffy_Sainte-Marie" title="Buffy Sainte-Marie">Buffy Sainte-Marie</a>, a Saskatchewan-born Cree folk singer who had inspired her. A year later, Mitchell too played Mariposa, her first gig for a major audience, and years later, Sainte-Marie herself covered her work.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1964.E2.80.931969:_Folk_breakthrough">1964–1969: Folk breakthrough</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1964–1969: Folk breakthrough">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ontario">Ontario</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ontario">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Lacking the $200 needed for musicians' union fees, Mitchell managed a few gigs at the Half Beat and the Village Corner in Toronto's <a href="/wiki/Yorkville,_Toronto" title="Yorkville, Toronto">Yorkville</a> neighbourhood, but mostly played non-union gigs "in church basements and YMCA meeting halls". Rejected from major folk clubs, she resorted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Busking" title="Busking">busking</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIGS_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-GIGS-22">[22]</a></sup> while she "worked in the women's wear section of a downtown department store to pay the rent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> During this era, she lived in a rooming house, directly across the hall from poet <a href="/wiki/Duke_Redbird" title="Duke Redbird">Duke Redbird</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dirtpoor_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-dirtpoor-26">[26]</a></sup> Without a lot of name recognition, Mitchell also began to realize each city's folk scene tended to accord veteran performers the exclusive right to play their signature songs—despite not having written the songs—which Mitchell found insular, contrary to the egalitarian ideal of folk music. She found her best traditional material was already other singers' property and would no longer pass muster. She said, "You'd come into a town and you'd be told, you can't sing that, you can't sing that." She resolved to write her own songs.</p>
<p>In late 1964, Mitchell discovered that she was pregnant by her Calgary ex-boyfriend Brad MacMath. She later wrote, "[he] left me three months pregnant in an attic room with no money and winter coming on and only a fireplace for heat. The spindles of the banister were gap-toothed fuel for last winter's occupants."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LINER_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-LINER-27">[27]</a></sup> At the time, "<a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">the pill</a>" was legally unavailable in Canada, as was abortion, yet there was a strong social stigma against women giving birth out of wedlock. In Toronto, she could at least do so quietly, without alarming her relatives back home. In February 1965 she gave birth to a baby girl. Unable to provide for the baby, she gave her daughter, Kelly Dale Anderson, up for adoption. The experience remained private for most of her career, but she made allusions to it in several songs, for example "<a href="/wiki/Little_Green_(song)" title="Little Green (song)">Little Green</a>", which she performed in the 1960s and eventually recorded for the 1971 album <i>Blue</i>.</p>
<p>In "Chinese Cafe", from the 1982 album <i>Wild Things Run Fast</i>, Mitchell sang, "Your kids are coming up straight / My child's a stranger / I bore her / But I could not raise her." These lyrics did not receive wide attention at the time. The existence of Mitchell's daughter was not publicly known until 1993, when a room-mate from Mitchell's art-school days in the 1960s sold the story of the adoption to a tabloid magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> By that time, Mitchell's daughter, renamed Kilauren Gibb, had already begun a search for her biological parents. Mitchell and her daughter met in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> After the reunion, Mitchell said that she lost interest in songwriting, and she later identified her daughter's birth and her inability to take care of her as the moment when her songwriting inspiration had really begun. When she could not express herself to the person she wanted to talk to, she became attuned to the whole world and she began to write personally.</p>
<p>A few weeks after the birth of her daughter in 1965, Joni Anderson was gigging again around Yorkville, beginning to sing more of her original material for the first time, written with her unique open tunings. In March and April she found work at the Penny Farthing, a folk club in Toronto. There she met Chuck Mitchell, an American folk-singer from <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>. Chuck was immediately attracted to her and impressed by her performance, and he told her that he could get her steady work in the coffeehouses he knew in the United States. In one interview, Mitchell claimed she married Chuck only 36 hours after they met, but it is unclear if they were ever married in Toronto. Sometime in late April, Mitchell left Canada for the first time, going with Chuck to the US, where the two began playing music together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIGS_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-GIGS-22">[22]</a></sup> Joni, 21 years old, married Chuck in an official ceremony in his hometown in June 1965 and took his surname. She said, "We had no money. I made my wedding dress ... I walked down the aisle brandishing my daisies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Michigan">Michigan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Michigan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>While living at the Verona apartments in Detroit's <a href="/wiki/Cass_Corridor" title="Cass Corridor">Cass Corridor</a>, Chuck and Joni were regular performers at area coffee houses, including the Alcove bar, near <a href="/wiki/Wayne_State_University" title="Wayne State University">Wayne State University</a>; the Rathskeller, a restaurant on the campus of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Detroit" title="University of Detroit">University of Detroit</a>; and the Raven Gallery in <a href="/wiki/Southfield,_Michigan" title="Southfield, Michigan">Southfield</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> She began playing and composing songs in alternative guitar tunings taught to her by a fellow musician, Eric Andersen, in Detroit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Brand" title="Oscar Brand">Oscar Brand</a> featured her several times on his CBC television program <i><a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Sing_Out" title="Let's Sing Out">Let's Sing Out</a></i> in 1965 and 1966, broadening her exposure. The marriage and partnership of Joni and Chuck Mitchell dissolved in early 1967, and Joni moved to New York City to pursue her musical dreams as a solo artist. She played venues up and down the East Coast, including <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Bragg,_North_Carolina" title="Fort Bragg, North Carolina">Fort Bragg, North Carolina</a>. She performed frequently in coffeehouses and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_clubs" title="Folk clubs">folk clubs</a> and, by this time creating her own material, became well known for her unique songwriting and her innovative guitar style.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York">New York</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: New York">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Folk singer <a href="/wiki/Tom_Rush" title="Tom Rush">Tom Rush</a> had met Mitchell in Toronto and was impressed with her songwriting ability. He took "Urge for Going" to the popular folk artist <a href="/wiki/Judy_Collins" title="Judy Collins">Judy Collins</a>, but she was not interested in the song at the time, so Rush recorded it himself. Country singer <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton_IV" title="George Hamilton IV">George Hamilton IV</a> heard Rush performing it and recorded a hit country version. Other artists who recorded Mitchell's songs in the early years were <a href="/wiki/Buffy_Sainte-Marie" title="Buffy Sainte-Marie">Buffy Sainte-Marie</a> ("The Circle Game"), <a href="/wiki/Dave_Van_Ronk" title="Dave Van Ronk">Dave Van Ronk</a> ("Both Sides Now"), and eventually <a href="/wiki/Judy_Collins" title="Judy Collins">Judy Collins</a> ("Both Sides Now", a top ten hit, and "Michael from Mountains", both included on her 1967 album <i><a href="/wiki/Wildflowers_(Judy_Collins_album)" title="Wildflowers (Judy Collins album)">Wildflowers</a></i>). Collins also covered "Chelsea Morning", a recording which again eclipsed Mitchell's own commercial success early on.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="California">California</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: California">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>While she was playing one night in the Gaslight South,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> a club in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coconut_Grove,_Miami,_Florida" title="Coconut Grove, Miami, Florida">Coconut Grove</a>, Florida, <a href="/wiki/David_Crosby" title="David Crosby">David Crosby</a> walked in and was immediately struck by her ability and her appeal as an artist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> He took her back to Los Angeles, where he set about introducing her and her music to his friends. Soon she was being managed by <a href="/wiki/Elliot_Roberts" title="Elliot Roberts">Elliot Roberts</a>, who had a close business association with <a href="/wiki/David_Geffen" title="David Geffen">David Geffen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Roberts and Geffen were to have important influences on her career. Crosby convinced a record company to let Mitchell record a solo acoustic album without the folk-rock overdubs in vogue at the time, and his clout earned him a producer's credit in March 1968, when <a href="/wiki/Reprise_Records" title="Reprise Records">Reprise Records</a> released her debut album, alternatively known as <i>Joni Mitchell</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Song_to_a_Seagull" title="Song to a Seagull">Song to a Seagull</a></i>.</p>
<p>Mitchell continued touring steadily to promote the LP. The tour helped create eager anticipation for Mitchell's second LP, <i><a href="/wiki/Clouds_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Clouds (Joni Mitchell album)">Clouds</a></i>, which was released in April 1969. This album contained Mitchell's own versions of some of her songs already recorded and performed by other artists: "<a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Morning" title="Chelsea Morning">Chelsea Morning</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Both_Sides,_Now" title="Both Sides, Now">Both Sides, Now</a>", and "Tin Angel". The covers of both LPs, including a self-portrait on <i><a href="/wiki/Clouds_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Clouds (Joni Mitchell album)">Clouds</a></i>, were designed and painted by Mitchell, a marriage of her art and music which she continued throughout her career.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970.E2.80.931974:_Mainstream_success">1970–1974: Mainstream success</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1970–1974: Mainstream success">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1941" data-file-width="1500" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg/170px-Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg/255px-Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg/340px-Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joni_mitchell_1974.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mitchell performing in concert at the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Amphitheatre" title="Universal Amphitheatre">Universal Amphitheatre</a> in August 1974</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In March 1970, <i><a href="/wiki/Clouds_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Clouds (Joni Mitchell album)">Clouds</a></i> produced her first <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Ethnic_or_Traditional_Folk_Recording" title="Grammy Award for Best Ethnic or Traditional Folk Recording">Grammy Award for Best Folk Performance</a>. The following month, Reprise released her third album, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ladies_of_the_Canyon" title="Ladies of the Canyon">Ladies of the Canyon</a></i>. Mitchell's sound was already beginning to expand beyond the confines of acoustic folk music and toward pop and rock, with more overdubs, percussion, and backing vocals, and for the first time, many songs composed on piano, which became a hallmark of Mitchell's style in her most popular era. Her own version of "<a href="/wiki/Woodstock_(song)" title="Woodstock (song)">Woodstock</a>", slower than the cover by <a href="/wiki/Crosby,_Stills,_Nash_%26_Young" title="Crosby, Stills, Nash &amp; Young">Crosby, Stills, Nash &amp; Young</a>, was performed solo on a <a href="/wiki/Wurlitzer_electric_piano" title="Wurlitzer electric piano">Wurlitzer electric piano</a>. The album also included the already-familiar song "The Circle Game" and the environmental anthem "<a href="/wiki/Big_Yellow_Taxi" title="Big Yellow Taxi">Big Yellow Taxi</a>", with its now-famous line, "they paved paradise and put up a parking lot."</p>
<p><i>Ladies of the Canyon</i> was an instant smash on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FM_radio" title="FM radio">FM radio</a> and sold briskly, eventually becoming Mitchell's first gold album (selling over a half million copies). She made a decision to stop touring for a year and just write and paint, yet she was still voted "Top Female Performer" for 1970 by <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i>, the UK's leading pop music magazine. On the April 1971 release of <a href="/wiki/James_Taylor" title="James Taylor">James Taylor</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mud_Slide_Slim_and_the_Blue_Horizon" title="Mud Slide Slim and the Blue Horizon">Mud Slide Slim and the Blue Horizon</a></i> album, Mitchell is credited with backup vocals – along with <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">Carole King</a> – on the track "<a href="/wiki/You%27ve_Got_a_Friend" title="You've Got a Friend">You've Got a Friend</a>". The songs she wrote during the months she took off for travel and life experience appeared on her next album, <i><a href="/wiki/Blue_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Blue (Joni Mitchell album)">Blue</a></i>, released in June 1971. Comparing Joni Mitchell's talent to his own, David Crosby said, "By the time she did <i>Blue</i>, she was past me and rushing toward the horizon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Blue</i> was an almost instant critical and commercial success, peaking in the top 20 in the Billboard Album Charts in September and also hitting the British Top 3. The lushly produced "<a href="/wiki/Carey_(song)" title="Carey (song)">Carey</a>" was the single at the time, but musically, other parts of <i>Blue</i> departed further from the sounds of <i>Ladies of the Canyon</i>. Simpler, rhythmic acoustic parts allowed a focus on Mitchell's voice and emotions ("All I Want", "<a href="/wiki/A_Case_of_You" title="A Case of You">A Case of You</a>"), while others such as "<a href="/wiki/Blue_(Joni_Mitchell_song)" title="Blue (Joni Mitchell song)">Blue</a>", "<a href="/wiki/River_(Joni_Mitchell_song)" title="River (Joni Mitchell song)">River</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/The_Last_Time_I_Saw_Richard" title="The Last Time I Saw Richard">The Last Time I Saw Richard</a>" were sung to her rolling piano accompaniment. Her most <a href="/wiki/Confessional_poetry" title="Confessional poetry">confessional</a> album, Mitchell later said of <i>Blue</i>, "I have, on occasion, sacrificed myself and my own emotional makeup, ... singing 'I'm selfish and I'm sad', for instance. We all suffer for our loneliness, but at the time of <i>Blue</i>, our pop stars never admitted these things."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shumway_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-shumway-38">[38]</a></sup> In its lyrics, the album was regarded as an inspired culmination of her early work, with depressed assessments of the world around her serving as counterpoint to exuberant expressions of romantic love (for example, in "<a href="/wiki/California_(Joni_Mitchell_song)" title="California (Joni Mitchell song)">California</a>"). Mitchell later remarked, "At that period of my life, I had no personal defenses. I felt like a cellophane wrapper on a pack of cigarettes. I felt like I had absolutely no secrets from the world and I couldn't pretend in my life to be strong."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crowe_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-crowe-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mitchell decided to return to the live stage after the great success of <i>Blue</i>, and she presented new songs on tour which appeared on her next album, her fifth, <i><a href="/wiki/For_the_Roses" title="For the Roses">For the Roses</a></i>. The album was released in October 1972 and immediately zoomed up the charts. She followed with the single, "<a href="/wiki/You_Turn_Me_On,_I%27m_a_Radio" title="You Turn Me On, I'm a Radio">You Turn Me On, I'm a Radio</a>", which peaked at No. 25 in the Billboard Charts in February 1973, becoming her first bona fide hit single.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Court_and_Spark" title="Court and Spark">Court and Spark</a></i>, released in January 1974, saw Mitchell begin the flirtation with <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jazz_fusion" title="Jazz fusion">jazz fusion</a> that marked her experimental period ahead. <i>Court and Spark</i> went to No. 1 on the Cashbox Album Charts. The LP made Mitchell a widely popular act for perhaps the only time in her career, on the strength of popular tracks such as the rocker "Raised on Robbery", which was released right before Christmas 1973, and "<a href="/wiki/Help_Me_(Joni_Mitchell_song)" title="Help Me (Joni Mitchell song)">Help Me</a>", which was released in March of the following year, and became Mitchell's only Top 10 single when it peaked at No. 7 in the first week of June. "<a href="/wiki/Free_Man_in_Paris" title="Free Man in Paris">Free Man in Paris</a>" was another hit single and staple in her catalog.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="781" data-file-width="893" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg/220px-1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg/330px-1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg/440px-1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1974_Joni_Mitchell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Publicity photo, 1974</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While recording <i>Court and Spark</i>, Mitchell had tried to make a clean break with her earlier folk sound, producing the album herself and employing jazz/pop <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_(music)" title="Fusion (music)">fusion</a> band the <a href="/wiki/L.A._Express" title="L.A. Express">L.A. Express</a> as what she called her first real backing group. In February 1974, her tour with the L.A. Express began, and they received rave notices as they traveled across the United States and Canada during the next two months. A series of shows at L.A.'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Amphitheater" title="Universal Amphitheater">Universal Amphitheater</a> on August 14–17 were recorded for a live album. In November, Mitchell released that album, <i><a href="/wiki/Miles_of_Aisles" title="Miles of Aisles">Miles of Aisles</a></i>, a two-record set including all but two songs from the L.A. concerts (one selection each from the <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Community_Theatre" title="Berkeley Community Theatre">Berkeley Community Theatre</a>, on March 2, and the L.A. Music Center, on March 4, were also included in the set). The live album slowly moved up to No. 2, matching <i>Court and Spark</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s chart peak on Billboard. "Big Yellow Taxi", the live version, was also released as a single and did reasonably well (she released another version of the song in 2007).</p>
<p>In January 1975, <i>Court and Spark</i> received four nominations for Grammy Awards, including <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Album_of_the_Year" title="Grammy Award for Album of the Year">Grammy Award for Album of the Year</a>, for which Mitchell was the only woman nominated. She won only the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Arrangement,_Instrumental_and_Vocals" title="Grammy Award for Best Arrangement, Instrumental and Vocals">Grammy Award for Best Arrangement, Instrumental and Vocals</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1975.E2.80.931980:_Jazz_explorations">1975–1980: Jazz explorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1975–1980: Jazz explorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joni_Mitchell_(1975).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="356" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Joni_Mitchell_%281975%29.png/220px-Joni_Mitchell_%281975%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Joni_Mitchell_%281975%29.png/330px-Joni_Mitchell_%281975%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Joni_Mitchell_%281975%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joni_Mitchell_(1975).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mitchell in 1975</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mitchell went into the studio in early 1975 to record acoustic demos of some songs that she had written since the <i>Court and Spark</i> tour. A few months later she recorded versions of the tunes with her band. Her musical interests were now diverging from both the folk and the pop scene of the era, toward less structured, more jazz-inspired pieces, with a wider range of instruments. The new song cycle was released in November 1975 as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hissing_of_Summer_Lawns" title="The Hissing of Summer Lawns">The Hissing of Summer Lawns</a></i>. On "The Jungle Line", she made an early effort at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_(music)" title="Sample (music)">sampling</a> a recording of African musicians, something that became more commonplace among Western rock acts in the 1980s. "In France They Kiss on Main Street" continued the lush pop sounds of <i>Court and Spark</i>, and efforts such as the title song and "Edith and the Kingpin" chronicled the underbelly of suburban lives in Southern California.</p>
<p>During 1975, Mitchell also participated in several concerts in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rolling_Thunder_Revue_tours" title="Rolling Thunder Revue tours">Rolling Thunder Revue tours</a> featuring <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">Joan Baez</a>, and in 1976 she performed as part of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Last_Waltz" title="The Last Waltz">The Last Waltz</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/The_Band" title="The Band">the Band</a>. In January 1976, Mitchell received a nomination for the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Female_Pop_Vocal_Performance" title="Grammy Award for Best Female Pop Vocal Performance">Grammy Award for Best Female Pop Vocal Performance</a> for the album <i>The Hissing of Summer Lawns</i>, though the Grammy went to <a href="/wiki/Linda_Ronstadt" title="Linda Ronstadt">Linda Ronstadt</a>.</p>
<p>In early 1976, Mitchell traveled with friends who were driving cross country to Maine. Afterwards, she drove back to California alone and composed several songs during her journey which featured on her next album, 1976's <i><a href="/wiki/Hejira_(album)" title="Hejira (album)">Hejira</a></i>. She stated that "This album was written mostly while I was traveling in the car. That's why there were no piano songs ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crowe_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-crowe-16">[16]</a></sup> <i>Hejira</i> was arguably Mitchell's most experimental album so far, due to her ongoing collaborations with jazz virtuoso bass guitarist <a href="/wiki/Jaco_Pastorius" title="Jaco Pastorius">Jaco Pastorius</a> on several songs, namely the first single, "<a href="/wiki/Coyote_(song)" title="Coyote (song)">Coyote</a>", the atmospheric "Hejira", the disorienting, guitar-heavy "Black Crow", and the album's last song "Refuge of the Roads". The album climbed to No. 13 on the Billboard Charts, reaching gold status three weeks after release, and received airplay from album oriented FM rock stations. Yet "Coyote", backed with "Blue Motel Room", failed to chart on the Hot 100. <i>Hejira</i> "did not sell as briskly as Mitchell's earlier, more "radio friendly" albums, [but] its stature in her catalogue has grown over the years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ottawa_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ottawa-39">[39]</a></sup> Mitchell herself believes the album to be unique. In 2006 she said, "I suppose a lot of people could have written a lot of my other songs, but I feel the songs on <i>Hejira</i> could only have come from me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ottawa_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-ottawa-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-1977, Mitchell began work on new recordings that became her first double studio album. Close to completing her contract with Asylum Records, Mitchell felt that this album could be looser in feel than any album she'd done in the past. She invited Pastorius back, and he brought with him fellow members of jazz fusion pioneers <a href="/wiki/Weather_Report" title="Weather Report">Weather Report</a>, including drummer Don Alias and saxophonist Wayne Shorter. Layered, atmospheric compositions such as "Overture/Cotton Avenue" featured more improvisatory collaboration, while "Paprika Plains" was a 16-minute epic that stretched the boundaries of pop, owing more to Mitchell's memories of childhood in Canada and her study of classical music. "Dreamland" and "The Tenth World", featuring <a href="/wiki/Chaka_Khan" title="Chaka Khan">Chaka Khan</a> on backing vocals, were percussion-dominated tracks. Other songs continued the jazz-rock-folk collisions of <i>Hejira</i>. Mitchell also revived "Jericho", written years earlier (a version is found on her 1974 live album) but never recorded in a studio setting. <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Juan%27s_Reckless_Daughter" title="Don Juan's Reckless Daughter">Don Juan's Reckless Daughter</a></i> was released in December 1977. The album received mixed reviews but still sold relatively well, peaking at No. 25 in the US and going gold within three months. The cover of the album created its own controversy: Mitchell was featured in several photographs, including one where she was disguised as a black man, wearing a curly afro wig, a white suit and vest, and dark sunglasses. The character, whom she called Art Nouveau, was based on a pimp who, she says, once complimented her while walking down an LA street – and was a symbol of her turn toward jazz and streetwise lyrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>A few months after the release of <i>Don Juan's Reckless Daughter</i>, Mitchell was contacted by the esteemed jazz composer, bandleader and bassist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mingus" title="Charles Mingus">Charles Mingus</a>, who had heard the orchestrated song "Paprika Plains", and wanted her to work with him. She began a collaboration with Mingus, who died before the project was completed in 1979. She finished the tracks, and the resulting album, <i><a href="/wiki/Mingus_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Mingus (Joni Mitchell album)">Mingus</a></i>, was released in June 1979, though it was poorly received in the press. Fans were confused over such a major change in Mitchell's overall sound, and though the album topped out at No. 17 on the Billboard album charts—a higher placement than <i>Don Juan's Reckless Daughter</i>—<i>Mingus</i> still fell short of gold status, making it her first album since the 1960s to not sell at least a half-million copies.</p>
<p>Mitchell's tour to promote <i>Mingus</i> began in August 1979 in Oklahoma City and concluded six weeks later with five shows at Los Angeles' <a href="/wiki/Greek_Theatre_(Los_Angeles)" title="Greek Theatre (Los Angeles)">Greek Theatre</a> and one at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Barbara_County_Bowl" title="Santa Barbara County Bowl">Santa Barbara County Bowl</a>, where she recorded and filmed the concert. It was her first tour in several years, and with Pastorius, jazz guitarist <a href="/wiki/Pat_Metheny" title="Pat Metheny">Pat Metheny</a>, and other members of her band, Mitchell also performed songs from her other jazz-inspired albums. When the tour ended she began a year of work, turning the tapes from the Santa Barbara County Bowl show into a two-album set and a concert film, both to be called <i><a href="/wiki/Shadows_and_Light_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Shadows and Light (Joni Mitchell album)">Shadows and Light</a></i>. Her final release on Asylum Records and her second live double-album, it was released in September 1980, and made it up to No. 38 on the Billboard Charts. A single from the LP, "Why Do Fools Fall in Love?", Mitchell's duet with <a href="/wiki/The_Persuasions" title="The Persuasions">The Persuasions</a> (her opening act for the tour), bubbled under on Billboard, just missing the Hot 100.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1981.E2.80.931993:_Pop.2C_electronics_and_protest">1981–1993: Pop, electronics and protest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1981–1993: Pop, electronics and protest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For a year and a half, Mitchell worked on the tracks for her next album. During this period she recorded with bassist <a href="/wiki/Larry_Klein" title="Larry Klein">Larry Klein</a>, whom she married in 1982. While the album was being readied for release, her friend David Geffen, founder of <a href="/wiki/Asylum_Records" title="Asylum Records">Asylum Records</a>, decided to start a new label, <a href="/wiki/Geffen_Records" title="Geffen Records">Geffen Records</a>. Still distributed by Warner Bros. (who controlled Asylum Records), Geffen negated the remaining contractual obligations Mitchell had with Asylum and signed her to his new label. <i><a href="/wiki/Wild_Things_Run_Fast" title="Wild Things Run Fast">Wild Things Run Fast</a></i> (1982) marked a return to pop songwriting, including "Chinese Cafe/<a href="/wiki/Unchained_Melody" title="Unchained Melody">Unchained Melody</a>", which incorporated the chorus and parts of the melody of the famous <a href="/wiki/The_Righteous_Brothers" title="The Righteous Brothers">The Righteous Brothers</a> hit, and "<a href="/wiki/(You%27re_So_Square)_Baby_I_Don%27t_Care" title="(You're So Square) Baby I Don't Care">(You're So Square) Baby I Don't Care</a>", a remake of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elvis" title="Elvis">Elvis</a> chestnut, which charted higher than any Mitchell single since her 1970s sales peak when it climbed to No. 47 on the charts. The album peaked on the Billboard Charts in its fifth week at No. 25.</p>
<p>In early 1983, Mitchell began a world tour, visiting Japan, Australia, Ireland, the United Kingdom, Belgium, France, Germany, Italy and Scandinavia and then going back to the United States. A performance from the tour was videotaped and later released on home video (and later DVD) as <i>Refuge of the Roads</i>. As 1984 ended, Mitchell was writing new songs, when she received a suggestion from Geffen that perhaps an outside producer with experience in the modern technical arenas that they wanted to explore might be a worthy addition. British <a href="/wiki/Synthpop" title="Synthpop">synthpop</a> performer and producer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Dolby" title="Thomas Dolby">Thomas Dolby</a> was brought on board. Of Dolby's role, Mitchell later commented: "I was reluctant when Thomas was suggested because he had been asked to produce the record [by Geffen], and would he consider coming in as just a programmer and a player? So on that level we did have some problems ... He may be able to do it faster. He may be able to do it better, but the fact is that it then wouldn't really be my music."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The album that resulted, <i><a href="/wiki/Dog_Eat_Dog_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Dog Eat Dog (Joni Mitchell album)">Dog Eat Dog</a></i>, released in October 1985, turned out to be only a moderate seller, peaking at No. 63 on Billboard's Top Albums Chart, Mitchell's lowest chart position since her first album peaked at No. 189 almost eighteen years before. One of the songs on the album, "Tax Free", created controversy by lambasting "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Televangelist" title="Televangelist">televangelists</a>" and what she saw as a drift to the <a href="/wiki/Christian_right" title="Christian right">religious right</a> in American politics. "The churches came after me", she wrote, "they attacked me, though the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopalian Church</a>, which I've seen described as the only church in America which actually uses its head, wrote me a letter of congratulation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irishtimes_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-irishtimes-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mitchell continued experimenting with synthesizers, drum machines and sequencers for the recordings of her next album, 1988's <i><a href="/wiki/Chalk_Mark_in_a_Rain_Storm" title="Chalk Mark in a Rain Storm">Chalk Mark in a Rain Storm</a></i>. She also collaborated with artists including <a href="/wiki/Willie_Nelson" title="Willie Nelson">Willie Nelson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Idol" title="Billy Idol">Billy Idol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wendy_%26_Lisa" title="Wendy &amp; Lisa">Wendy &amp; Lisa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Petty" title="Tom Petty">Tom Petty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Don_Henley" title="Don Henley">Don Henley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Gabriel" title="Peter Gabriel">Peter Gabriel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Orr" title="Benjamin Orr">Benjamin Orr</a> of the <a href="/wiki/The_Cars" title="The Cars">Cars</a>. The album's first official single, "My Secret Place", was in fact a duet with Gabriel, and just missed the Billboard Hot 100 charts. The song "Lakota" was one of many songs on the album to take on larger political themes, in this case the <a href="/wiki/Wounded_Knee_incident" title="Wounded Knee incident">Wounded Knee incident</a>, the deadly battle between Native American activists and the FBI on <a href="/wiki/Pine_Ridge_Indian_Reservation" title="Pine Ridge Indian Reservation">Pine Ridge Indian Reservation</a> in the previous decade. Musically, several songs fit into the trend of <a href="/wiki/World_music" title="World music">world music</a> popularized by Gabriel during the era. Reviews were mostly favorable towards the album, and the cameos by well-known musicians brought it considerable attention. <i>Chalk Mark</i> ultimately improved on the chart performance of <i>Dog Eat Dog</i>, peaking at No. 45.</p>
<p>In 1990, Mitchell, who by then rarely performed live anymore, participated in <a href="/wiki/Roger_Waters" title="Roger Waters">Roger Waters</a>' <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Wall_Concert_in_Berlin" title="The Wall Concert in Berlin">The Wall Concert in Berlin</a></i>. She performed the song "<a href="/wiki/Goodbye_Blue_Sky" title="Goodbye Blue Sky">Goodbye Blue Sky</a>" and was also one of the performers on the concert's final song "<a href="/wiki/The_Tide_Is_Turning" title="The Tide Is Turning">The Tide Is Turning</a>" along with Waters, <a href="/wiki/Cyndi_Lauper" title="Cyndi Lauper">Cyndi Lauper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Adams" title="Bryan Adams">Bryan Adams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Van_Morrison" title="Van Morrison">Van Morrison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Carrack" title="Paul Carrack">Paul Carrack</a>.</p>
<p>Throughout the first half of 1990, Mitchell recorded songs that appeared on her next album. She delivered the final mixes for the new album to Geffen just before Christmas, after trying nearly a hundred different sequences for the songs. The album <i><a href="/wiki/Night_Ride_Home" title="Night Ride Home">Night Ride Home</a></i> was released in March 1991. In the United States, it premiered on Billboard's Top Album charts at No. 68, moving up to No. 48 in its second week, and peaking at No. 41 in its sixth week. In the United Kingdom, the album premiered at No. 25 on the album charts. Critically, it was better received than her 1980s work and seemed to signal a move closer to her acoustic beginnings, along with some references to the style of <i>Hejira</i>. This album was also Mitchell's first since Geffen Records was sold to <a href="/wiki/MCA_Inc." title="MCA Inc.">MCA Inc.</a>, meaning that <i>Night Ride Home</i> was her first album not to be initially distributed by WEA (now <a href="/wiki/Warner_Music_Group" title="Warner Music Group">Warner Music Group</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1994.E2.80.932001:_Resurgence_and_vocal_development">1994–2001: Resurgence and vocal development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1994–2001: Resurgence and vocal development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To wider audiences, the real "return to form" for Mitchell came with 1994's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammy" title="Grammy">Grammy</a>-winning <i><a href="/wiki/Turbulent_Indigo" title="Turbulent Indigo">Turbulent Indigo</a></i>. While the recording period also saw the divorce of Mitchell and bassist <a href="/wiki/Larry_Klein" title="Larry Klein">Larry Klein</a>, their marriage having lasted almost 12 years, <i>Indigo</i> was seen as Mitchell's most accessible set of songs in years. Songs such as "Sex Kills", "Sunny Sunday", "Borderline" and "The Magdalene Laundries" mixed social commentary and guitar-focused melodies for "a startling comeback".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toronto_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toronto-42">[42]</a></sup> The album won two Grammy awards, including Best Pop Album, and it coincided with a much-publicized resurgence in interest in Mitchell's work by a younger generation of singer-songwriters.</p>
<p>In 1996, Mitchell agreed to release a greatest <i><a href="/wiki/Hits_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Hits (Joni Mitchell album)">Hits</a></i> collection when label Reprise also allowed her a second <i><a href="/wiki/Misses_(album)" title="Misses (album)">Misses</a></i> album to include some of the lesser known songs from her career. <i>Hits</i> charted at No. 161 in the US, but made No. 6 in the UK. Mitchell also included on <i>Hits</i>, for the first time on an album, her first recording, a version of "Urge for Going" which preceded <i>Song to a Seagull</i> but was previously released only as a B-side.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="1614" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg/220px-Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg/330px-Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg/440px-Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joni_Mitchell_and_Peter_Bogner.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Joni Mitchell and Peter Bogner listening to premix of <a href="/wiki/Herbie_Hancock" title="Herbie Hancock">Herbie Hancock's</a> Gershwin's World (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venice_Beach,_California" title="Venice Beach, California">Venice Beach, California</a> in 1999)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two years later, Mitchell released her final set of "original" new work before nearly a decade of other pursuits, 1998's <i><a href="/wiki/Taming_the_Tiger" title="Taming the Tiger">Taming the Tiger</a></i>. She promoted <i>Tiger</i> with a return to regular concert appearances, including a co-headlining tour with <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Van_Morrison" title="Van Morrison">Van Morrison</a>. On the album, Mitchell had played a "<a href="/wiki/Guitar_synthesizer" title="Guitar synthesizer">guitar synthesizer</a>" on most songs, and for the tour she adapted many of her old songs to this instrument, and reportedly had to re-learn all her complex tunings once again.</p>
<p>It was around this time that critics also began to notice a real change in Mitchell's voice, particularly on her older songs; the singer later admitted to feeling the same way, explaining that "I'd go to hit a note and there was nothing there".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renai_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-renai-43">[43]</a></sup> While her more limited range and huskier vocals have sometimes been attributed to her smoking (she has been described as "one of the world's last great smokers"), Mitchell believes that the changes in her voice that became noticeable in the 1990s were due to other problems, including vocal nodules, a compressed larynx, and the lingering effects of having had polio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renai_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-renai-43">[43]</a></sup> In an interview in 2004, she denied that "my terrible habits" had anything to do with her more limited range and pointed out that singers often lose the upper register when they pass fifty. In addition, she contended that in her opinion her voice became a more interesting and expressive alto range when she could no longer hit the high notes, let alone hold them like she did in her youth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>The singer's next two albums featured no new songs and, Mitchell has said, were recorded to "fulfill contractual obligations",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toronto_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Toronto-42">[42]</a></sup> but on both she attempted to make use of her new vocal range in interpreting familiar material. <i><a href="/wiki/Both_Sides_Now_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Both Sides Now (Joni Mitchell album)">Both Sides Now</a></i> (2000) was an album composed mostly of covers of jazz standards, performed with an orchestra, featuring orchestral arrangements by <a href="/wiki/Vince_Mendoza" title="Vince Mendoza">Vince Mendoza</a>. The album also contained remakes of "A Case of You" and the title track "Both Sides Now", two early hits transposed down to Mitchell's now dusky, soulful alto range. It received mostly strong reviews and spawned a short national tour, with Mitchell accompanied by a core band featuring Larry Klein on bass plus a local orchestra on each tour stop. Its success led to 2002's <i><a href="/wiki/Travelogue_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Travelogue (Joni Mitchell album)">Travelogue</a></i>, a collection of re-workings of her previous songs with lush orchestral accompaniments.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2002.E2.80.932005:_Retirement_and_retrospectives">2002–2005: Retirement and retrospectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 2002–2005: Retirement and retrospectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mitchell stated at the time that <i>Travelogue</i> would be her final album. In a 2002 interview with <i>Rolling Stone</i>, she voiced discontent with the current state of the music industry, describing it as a "cesspool".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wild_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-wild-2">[2]</a></sup> Mitchell expressed her dislike of the record industry's dominance and her desire to control her own destiny, possibly by releasing her own music over the Internet.</p>
<p>During the next few years, the only albums Mitchell released were compilations of her earlier work. In 2003, her Geffen recordings were collected in a remastered four-disc box set, <i>The Complete Geffen Recordings</i>, including notes by Mitchell and three previously unreleased tracks. A series of themed compilations of songs from earlier albums were also released: <i>The Beginning of Survival</i> (2004), <i><a href="/wiki/Dreamland_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Dreamland (Joni Mitchell album)">Dreamland</a></i> (2004), and <i>Songs of a Prairie Girl</i> (2005), the last of which collected the threads of her Canadian upbringing and which she released after accepting an invitation to the <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_Medal_for_the_Centennial_of_Saskatchewan" title="Commemorative Medal for the Centennial of Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan Centennial</a> concert in Saskatoon. The concert, which featured a tribute to Mitchell, was also attended by <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a>. In the <i>Prairie Girl</i> liner notes, she writes that the collection is "my contribution to Saskatchewan's Centennial celebrations".</p>
<p>In the early 1990s, Mitchell signed a deal with <a href="/wiki/Random_House" title="Random House">Random House</a> to publish an autobiography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In 1998 she told <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> that her memoirs were "in the works", that they would be published in as many as four volumes, and that the first line would be "I was the only black man at the party."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In 2005, Mitchell said that she was using a tape recorder to get her memories "down in the oral tradition".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nymag_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-nymag-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Mitchell stated that she would no longer tour or give concerts, she has made occasional public appearances to speak on environmental issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Mitchell divides her time between her longtime home in Los Angeles, and the 80-acre (32 ha) property in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sechelt,_British_Columbia" title="Sechelt, British Columbia">Sechelt, British Columbia</a>, that she has owned since the early 1970s. "L.A. is my workplace", she said in 2006, "B.C. is my heartbeat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> According to interviews, today she focuses mainly on her visual art, which she does not sell and displays only on rare occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2006.E2.80.932015:_Late_recordings">2006–2015: Late recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 2006–2015: Late recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In an interview with the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ottawa_Citizen" title="The Ottawa Citizen">Ottawa Citizen</a></i> in October 2006, Mitchell "revealed that she was recording her first collection of new songs in nearly a decade", but gave few other details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ottawa_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-ottawa-39">[39]</a></sup> Four months later, in an interview with the <i>New York Times</i>, Mitchell said that the forthcoming album, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Shine_(Joni_Mitchell_album)" title="Shine (Joni Mitchell album)">Shine</a></i>, was inspired by the war in Iraq and "something her grandson had said while listening to family fighting: 'Bad dreams are good—in the great plan.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytdance_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytdance-51">[51]</a></sup> Early media reports characterized the album as having "a minimal feel ... that harks back to [Mitchell's] early work" and a focus on political and environmental issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renai_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-renai-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2007, Mitchell returned to <a href="/wiki/Calgary" title="Calgary">Calgary</a> and served as an advisor for the <a href="/wiki/Alberta_Ballet_Company" title="Alberta Ballet Company">Alberta Ballet Company</a> premiere of "The Fiddle and the Drum", a dance choreographed to both new and old songs by Jean Grand-Maitre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> She worked with the French-Canadian TV director Mario Rouleau, well known for work in art and dance for television, such as <a href="/wiki/Cirque_du_Soleil" title="Cirque du Soleil">Cirque du Soleil</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> She also filmed portions of the rehearsals for a documentary that she is working on. Of the flurry of recent activity she quipped, "I've never worked so hard in my life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytdance_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytdance-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-2007, Mitchell's official fan-run site confirmed speculation that she had signed a two-record deal with <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">Starbucks</a>' <a href="/wiki/Hear_Music" title="Hear Music">Hear Music</a> label. <i>Shine</i> was released by the label on September 25, 2007, debuting at number 14 on the Billboard 200 album chart, her highest chart position in the United States since the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Hejira_(album)" title="Hejira (album)">Hejira</a></i> in 1976, over thirty years previously, and at number 36 on the United Kingdom albums chart.</p>
<p>On the same day, <a href="/wiki/Herbie_Hancock" title="Herbie Hancock">Herbie Hancock</a>, a longtime associate and friend of Mitchell's, released <i><a href="/wiki/River:_The_Joni_Letters" title="River: The Joni Letters">River: The Joni Letters</a></i>, an album paying tribute to Mitchell's work. Among the album's contributors were <a href="/wiki/Norah_Jones" title="Norah Jones">Norah Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tina_Turner" title="Tina Turner">Tina Turner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Cohen" title="Leonard Cohen">Leonard Cohen</a>, and Mitchell herself, who contributed a vocal to the re-recording of "The Tea Leaf Prophecy (Lay Down Your Arms)" (originally on her album <i><a href="/wiki/Chalk_Mark_in_a_Rain_Storm" title="Chalk Mark in a Rain Storm">Chalk Mark in a Rain Storm</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> On February 10, 2008, Hancock's recording won Album of the Year at the Grammy Awards. It was the first time in 43 years that a jazz artist took the top prize at the annual award ceremony. In accepting the award, Hancock paid tribute to Mitchell as well as to <a href="/wiki/Miles_Davis" title="Miles Davis">Miles Davis</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Coltrane" title="John Coltrane">John Coltrane</a>. At the same ceremony Mitchell won a Grammy for Best Instrumental Pop Performance for the opening track, "One Week Last Summer", from her album <i>Shine</i>.</p>
<p>In a 2010 interview with the <i>Los Angeles Times</i>, Mitchell was quoted as saying that singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a>, with whom she had worked closely in the past, was a fake and a <a href="/wiki/Plagiarism" title="Plagiarism">plagiarist</a>. The controversial remark was widely reported by other media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell:_Dylan.27s_a_fake_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell:_Dylan.27s_a_fake-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Mitchell did not explain the contention further, but several media outlets speculated that it may have related to the allegations of plagiarism surrounding some lyrics on Dylan's 2006 album <i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Times_(Bob_Dylan_album)" title="Modern Times (Bob Dylan album)">Modern Times</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell:_Dylan.27s_a_fake_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell:_Dylan.27s_a_fake-55">[55]</a></sup> In a 2013 interview with <a href="/wiki/Jian_Ghomeshi" title="Jian Ghomeshi">Jian Ghomeshi</a>, she was asked about the comments and responded by denying that she had made the statement while mentioning the allegations of plagiarism that arose over the lyrics to Dylan's 2001 album <i><a href="/wiki/Love_and_Theft_(Bob_Dylan_album)" title="Love and Theft (Bob Dylan album)">Love and Theft</a></i> in the general context of the flow and ebb of the creative process of artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morgellons">Morgellons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Morgellons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mitchell has claimed that she has <a href="/wiki/Morgellons" title="Morgellons">Morgellons syndrome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> the informal name given to a self-diagnosed skin condition that is better explained as <a href="/wiki/Delusional_parasitosis" title="Delusional parasitosis">delusional infestation</a> in a majority of cases, according to a study conducted by the CDC as well as general consensus within the medical community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Mitchell spoke to the <i>Los Angeles Times</i> in 2010 about Morgellons, saying, "I have this weird, incurable disease that seems like it's from outer space, but my health's the best it's been in a while." She said at that time that she planned to leave the music industry to work toward giving more credibility to people diagnosed with Morgellons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brain_aneurysm">Brain aneurysm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Brain aneurysm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On March 31, 2015, Mitchell was found unconscious in her Los Angeles home. She regained consciousness in an ambulance on her way to hospital, but was taken to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive_care" title="Intensive care">intensive care</a> for tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Since then, there have been conflicting reports about her condition. On April 28, 2015, an official statement was made through JoniMitchell.com:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Contrary to rumors circulating on the Internet today, Joni is not in a coma. Joni is still in the hospital – but she comprehends, she's alert, and she has her full senses. A full recovery is expected. The document obtained by a certain media outlet simply gives her longtime friend Leslie Morris the authority – in the absence of 24-hour doctor care – to make care decisions for Joni once she leaves the hospital. As we all know, Joni is a strong-willed woman and is nowhere near giving up the fight. Please continue to keep Joni in your thoughts.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Her longtime friend Leslie Morris invited get-well wishes at a website called "We Love You, Joni!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In early May, Mitchell's lawyer released a statement during a court hearing concerning a request for conservatorship from Morris, stating that Mitchell would soon be released from the hospital and returning home; but that did not immediately occur.</p>
<p>On May 29, 2015, it was confirmed that Mitchell had suffered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brain_aneurysm" title="Brain aneurysm">brain aneurysm</a> and that while speech was difficult, she had been communicating with others. As of May 2015 Mitchell was expected to be moved to a rehabilitation facility, as her condition was still considered to be "very serious".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> About a month later close friend <a href="/wiki/David_Crosby" title="David Crosby">David Crosby</a> said "nobody found her for a while" and "to my knowledge, she is not speaking yet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> However, Mitchell's conservator, Leslie Morris, later released a statement saying that "details that have emerged in the past few days are mostly speculative. The truth is that Joni is speaking, and she's speaking well. She is not walking yet..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2015, Mitchell was back at home, undergoing physical therapy and "making progress", according to her lawyer Rebecca J. Thyne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In October 2015, Mitchell's friend, singer <a href="/wiki/Judy_Collins" title="Judy Collins">Judy Collins</a>, reported that she was taking part in rehabilitation every day and was walking, talking and painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Joni_Mitchell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
